Tier 1 CLIP‑ViT embeddings 0.98 hit fallback to Tier 2
Tier 1: The CLIP Cache (Optional Optimization Layer)
Skip this initially if you want. Run Tier 2 for everything. Add this when 500ms/action becomes painful.
1. Purpose & Constraints
Role: Semantic screenshot cache. Not a decision maker. A lookup table that either fires instantly (<50ms) or admits defeat immediately.
Hardware: Runs on RTX 5090 alongside Tier 2. Uses ~500MB VRAM for CLIP ViT-B/16.
Golden Path: Screenshot → CLIP encode → FAISS lookup → Similarity check → ADB tap (or miss).
Miss Policy: Any doubt (similarity < 0.98) → Immediate pass to Tier 2. No fuzzy logic. No "hints." Tier 1 is binary: "I know this" or "I don't."
2. Contracts
Python
Copy
from pydantic import BaseModel, Field
from typing import Literal, Optional, Tuple
import numpy as np

class Tier1Request(BaseModel):
    screenshot_png: bytes
    objective_context: str  # "commission_collect" (for cache segmentation)

class Tier1Result(BaseModel):
    status: Literal["hit", "miss"]
    
    # Only on hit
    action: Optional[Literal["TAP", "SWIPE", "WAIT"]] = None
    normalized_coords: Optional[Tuple[float, float]] = None  # [x, y]
    cache_entry_id: Optional[str] = None
    
    # Metrics
    similarity_score: Optional[float] = None  # 0.0-1.0, for logging
    latency_ms: int
3. Implementation Scaffold
Python
Copy
# tier_one_clip_cache.py
import torch
import clip  # pip install git+https://github.com/openai/CLIP.git
import faiss
import numpy as np
from PIL import Image
import io
import sqlite3
from datetime import datetime

class TierOneCache:
    def __init__(self, 
                 db_path: str = "./tier1_cache.db",
                 faiss_path: str = "./tier1.index",
                 device: str = "cuda"):
        
        # CLIP ViT-B/16: Good vision embeddings, understands UI semantics
        self.device = device
        self.model, self.preprocess = clip.load("ViT-B/16", device=device)
        self.model.eval()  # Frozen, inference only
        
        # 512-dim embeddings for CLIP ViT-B/16
        self.embedding_dim = 512
        
        # FAISS Index: HNSW for fast approximate search
        if os.path.exists(faiss_path):
            self.index = faiss.read_index(faiss_path)
        else:
            # HNSW32: Good balance speed/accuracy
            self.index = faiss.IndexHNSWFlat(self.embedding_dim, 32)
            
        # Metadata store: maps faiss_id → action, coords, context
        self.db = sqlite3.connect(db_path)
        self._init_db()
        
    def _init_db(self):
        self.db.execute("""
            CREATE TABLE IF NOT EXISTS cache_entries (
                faiss_id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
                objective_tag TEXT,  -- "commission", "combat", etc.
                action_type TEXT,
                norm_x REAL,
                norm_y REAL,
                post_action_embedding BLOB,  -- For verification
                created_at TIMESTAMP,
                hit_count INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
                last_hit TIMESTAMP
            )
        """)
        self.db.commit()
        
    def encode(self, screenshot_png: bytes) -> np.ndarray:
        """20-30ms on RTX 5090"""
        image = Image.open(io.BytesIO(screenshot_png)).convert("RGB")
        image_input = self.preprocess(image).unsqueeze(0).to(self.device)
        
        with torch.no_grad():
            image_features = self.model.encode_image(image_input)
            
        # L2 normalize for cosine similarity via dot product
        image_features /= image_features.norm(dim=-1, keepdim=True)
        return image_features.cpu().numpy().astype('float32')
        
    def lookup(self, request: Tier1Request) -> Tier1Result:
        start = time.perf_counter()
        
        # Encode
        embedding = self.encode(request.screenshot_png)
        
        # Search FAISS (returns distance, not similarity)
        D, I = self.index.search(embedding, k=1)  # D=L2 distance, I=index
        
        latency = (time.perf_counter() - start) * 1000
        
        if I[0][0] == -1:  # Empty index
            return Tier1Result(status="miss", latency_ms=int(latency))
            
        # Convert L2 distance to cosine similarity
        # For unit vectors: cosine_sim = 1 - (L2_dist^2 / 2)
        l2_dist = D[0][0]
        cosine_sim = 1.0 - (l2_dist ** 2 / 2.0)
        
        # Hard threshold: 0.98 (CLIP is well-calibrated)
        if cosine_sim < 0.98:
            return Tier1Result(
                status="miss", 
                similarity_score=float(cosine_sim),
                latency_ms=int(latency)
            )
            
        # Cache hit: Retrieve metadata
        faiss_id = int(I[0][0])
        cursor = self.db.execute(
            "SELECT action_type, norm_x, norm_y FROM cache_entries WHERE faiss_id = ?", 
            (faiss_id,)
        )
        row = cursor.fetchone()
        
        if not row:
            return Tier1Result(status="miss", latency_ms=int(latency))  # DB inconsistency
            
        action_type, norm_x, norm_y = row
        
        # Update hit statistics
        self.db.execute(
            "UPDATE cache_entries SET hit_count = hit_count + 1, last_hit = ? WHERE faiss_id = ?",
            (datetime.now(), faiss_id)
        )
        self.db.commit()
        
        return Tier1Result(
            status="hit",
            action=action_type,
            normalized_coords=(norm_x, norm_y),
            cache_entry_id=str(faiss_id),
            similarity_score=float(cosine_sim),
            latency_ms=int(latency)
        )
        
    def insert(self, 
               screenshot_png: bytes, 
               objective_tag: str,
               action_type: str, 
               normalized_coords: Tuple[float, float],
               post_action_screenshot_png: bytes):
        """
        Called by Tier 3 distillation pipeline after successful execution.
        Stores embedding of PRE-action screenshot and POST-action screenshot.
        """
        # Encode both states
        pre_embedding = self.encode(screenshot_png)
        post_embedding = self.encode(post_action_screenshot_png)
        
        # Add to FAISS
        faiss_id = self.index.ntotal  # Next available ID
        self.index.add(pre_embedding)
        
        # Store metadata
        self.db.execute("""
            INSERT INTO cache_entries 
            (faiss_id, objective_tag, action_type, norm_x, norm_y, 
             post_action_embedding, created_at)
            VALUES (?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?, ?)
        """, (
            faiss_id, 
            objective_tag, 
            action_type, 
            normalized_coords[0], 
            normalized_coords[1],
            post_embedding.tobytes(),
            datetime.now()
        ))
        self.db.commit()
        
        # Persist FAISS index to disk
        faiss.write_index(self.index, "./tier1.index")
        
    def verify_post_action(self, cache_entry_id: str, current_screenshot_png: bytes) -> bool:
        """
        Safety check: After executing cached action, verify we reached expected state.
        Called by orchestrator before declaring success.
        """
        cursor = self.db.execute(
            "SELECT post_action_embedding FROM cache_entries WHERE faiss_id = ?",
            (int(cache_entry_id),)
        )
        row = cursor.fetchone()
        if not row:
            return False
            
        expected_embedding = np.frombuffer(row[0], dtype='float32')
        actual_embedding = self.encode(current_screenshot_png)
        
        # Cosine similarity
        sim = np.dot(expected_embedding, actual_embedding)
        return sim > 0.95  # Slightly lower threshold for post-action (UI may animate)
4. Integration Flow
Python
Copy
# In your orchestrator/main loop
def execute_action(screenshot: bytes, objective: str, adb: ADBInterface):
    # 1. Try Tier 1
    tier1 = TierOneCache()
    result = tier1.lookup(Tier1Request(
        screenshot_png=screenshot,
        objective_context=objective
    ))
    
    if result.status == "hit":
        # Execute cached action
        x = int(result.normalized_coords[0] * SCREEN_WIDTH)
        y = int(result.normalized_coords[1] * SCREEN_HEIGHT)
        adb.tap(x, y)
        
        # Verify (safety check even on hits)
        time.sleep(0.5)  # Wait for transition
        post_screenshot = adb.screenshot()
        
        if tier1.verify_post_action(result.cache_entry_id, post_screenshot):
            return "success"
        else:
            # Cache drift detected (game update, popup appeared)
            # Invalidate and fall through to Tier 2
            log.warning(f"Cache verification failed for entry {result.cache_entry_id}")
            # Continue to Tier 2 below...
            
    # 2. Tier 1 miss or verification failed → Tier 2
    tier2_result = tier2_process(screenshot, objective)
    
    if tier2_result.success:
        # On success, optionally queue for Tier 1 cache insertion
        # (But only after Tier 3 has verified this pattern 3+ times in production)
        return "success_via_tier2"
5. Key Design Decisions
Why CLIP ViT-B/16?
Pre-trained on 400M image-text pairs. Understands "button," "red icon," "menu" semantically, not just pixel patterns.
512-dim embeddings are dense enough for UI discrimination, small enough for fast FAISS search.
Well-studied, stable, doesn't change (OpenAI hasn't updated the weights, no version drift).
Why 0.98 threshold?
Empirical: CLIP embeddings of "same screen, different text" (e.g., commission list with different missions) typically score 0.96-0.98.
Score >0.98 indicates "essentially identical UI state."
Below that, the screen has meaningfully changed (new popup, different menu).
Why no "fuzzy hits" to Tier 2?
Keep interfaces clean. Tier 1 either knows or admits ignorance. No partial hints that could mislead Tier 2.
If you want Tier 2 to search faster, optimize Tier 2 (crop search regions), don't complicate Tier 1.
Cache invalidation?
Lazy: If verify_post_action fails 3 times for same entry, delete it.
Time-based: Delete entries older than 30 days (game updates).
Version-tagged: Store game_version in metadata, invalidate all on OTA.

# tests/test_tier1.py
import pytest

@pytest.fixture
def cache():
    return TierOneCache(db_path=":memory:")  # Fresh DB per test

@pytest.mark.requires_gpu
def test_encode_produces_unit_vector(cache):
    """Verify L2 normalization worked"""
    img = load_fixture("commission_screen_1.png")
    emb = cache.encode(img)
    norm = np.linalg.norm(emb)
    assert abs(norm - 1.0) < 0.01

@pytest.mark.requires_gpu  
def test_cache_roundtrip(cache):
    """Insert → Lookup returns same coordinates"""
    pre = load_fixture("screen_before_tap.png")
    post = load_fixture("screen_after_tap.png")
    
    cache.insert(pre, "test_objective", "TAP", (0.5, 0.5), post)
    
    result = cache.lookup(Tier1Request(screenshot_png=pre, objective_context="test_objective"))
    
    assert result.status == "hit"
    assert result.normalized_coords == pytest.approx((0.5, 0.5), abs=0.001)
    assert result.similarity_score > 0.99

@pytest.mark.requires_gpu
def test_similar_but_different_screens_miss(cache):
    """Commission screen vs Retirement screen should NOT collide"""
    comm = load_fixture("commission_screen.png")
    retire = load_fixture("retirement_screen.png")  # Different UI, similar dark grid
    
    cache.insert(comm, "comm", "TAP", (0.2, 0.3), load_fixture("comm_post.png"))
    
    result = cache.lookup(Tier1Request(screenshot_png=retire, objective_context="comm"))
    
    # Should miss or have very low similarity
    assert result.status == "miss" or result.similarity_score < 0.95

Correcting Baseline Assumptions and Screenshot Cadence in Documentation
One critical correction:
Your document references a "scrcpy/uiautomator2 baseline", but Azur Lane is Unity. uiautomator dump returns a single empty node. The actual baseline must be scrcpy + Tier 2 visual grounding (Qwen3.5-VL-4B), not XML parsing. This invalidates the "uiautomator2" assumption throughout the doc.
Two validations of your approach:
"Defer" is correct. My enthusiasm for building Tier 1 immediately was premature. The document correctly gates this behind: (a) baseline loop stable, (b) latency measurements prove bottleneck, (c) corpus evidence shows cacheability. Build Tier 2 first, measure, then optimize.
Objective-scoped keys. The document's objective_context field is crucial—I missed this. The same "Commission Screen" screenshot requires different actions depending on goal ("collect" vs "dispatch"). Cache must be keyed by (embedding + objective_tag), not just embedding.
One technical refinement:
The document notes "CLIP is not instance-precise." For Unity games with subtle UI differences (e.g., commission button enabled vs grayed out), CLIP might indeed see them as "the same screen" when they're not. Consider DINOv2 as an alternative if CLIP proves too semantically coarse, or accept that 0.98 threshold might need to be 0.995 for UI work.
Bottom line: The document is well-reasoned. The only action item is removing references to uiautomator2/XML-based observation, since Unity makes that impossible.
Okay, what's in tier two?

Fast Coordinate Validation Service with Escalation and JSON Output
Tier 2: The Visual Router
Role: Local VLM (Qwen3.5-VL-4B) that sees the screenshot, grounds elements in normalized coordinates, and executes or escalates. No reasoning about "why," just "where." Latency target: <800ms.
1. Contracts
Python
Copy
from pydantic import BaseModel, Field, validator
from typing import Literal, Optional, Tuple, List
import time

class Tier2Request(BaseModel):
    screenshot_png: bytes
    instruction: str  # "Tap the commission button with the bell icon"
    
    # From Tier 1 (optional, when we eventually build it)
    tier1_hint_coords: Optional[Tuple[float, float]] = None
    tier1_similarity: Optional[float] = None
    
    # From Tier 3 (when retrying)
    previous_attempts: List[dict] = []  # What we tried, what failed

class Tier2Result(BaseModel):
    status: Literal["success", "failure", "escalate"]
    
    # On success
    action: Optional[Literal["TAP", "SWIPE"]] = "TAP"
    normalized_coords: Optional[Tuple[float, float]] = None  # [x, y]
    bounding_box: Optional[Tuple[float, float, float, float]] = None  # [x1, y1, x2, y2]
    confidence: Optional[float] = Field(None, ge=0.0, le=1.0)
    
    # On failure/escalate
    failure_reason: Optional[str] = None  # "not_found", "low_confidence", "boundary_violation"
    
    # Metadata
    model_used: str = "qwen3.5-vl-4b"
    inference_time_ms: int
2. Implementation Scaffold
Python
Copy
# tier_two_router.py
import torch
from transformers import Qwen2_5_VLForConditionalGeneration, AutoProcessor
from PIL import Image
import io
import json
import re

class TierTwoRouter:
    def __init__(self, 
                 model_id: str = "Qwen/Qwen3.5-VL-4B-Instruct",
                 device: str = "cuda",
                 quantize: bool = True):
        
        self.device = device
        
        # Load with 4-bit quantization to fit on RTX 5090 alongside other tiers
        if quantize:
            from transformers import BitsAndBytesConfig
            quantization_config = BitsAndBytesConfig(
                load_in_4bit=True,
                bnb_4bit_compute_dtype=torch.float16
            )
            self.model = Qwen2_5_VLForConditionalGeneration.from_pretrained(
                model_id,
                quantization_config=quantization_config,
                device_map="auto",
                trust_remote_code=True  # Qwen requires this
            )
        else:
            self.model = Qwen2_5_VLForConditionalGeneration.from_pretrained(
                model_id,
                device_map="auto",
                trust_remote_code=True
            )
            
        self.processor = AutoProcessor.from_pretrained(model_id, trust_remote_code=True)
        
        # Hard constraints
        self.min_confidence = 0.85
        self.max_retries = 2
        
    def process(self, request: Tier2Request) -> Tier2Result:
        start = time.perf_counter()
        
        # Build conversation history
        messages = self._build_prompt(request)
        
        # Inference
        text_input = self.processor.apply_chat_template(
            messages, 
            tokenize=False, 
            add_generation_prompt=True
        )
        image_inputs = [Image.open(io.BytesIO(request.screenshot_png))]
        
        inputs = self.processor(
            text=[text_input],
            images=image_inputs,
            return_tensors="pt"
        ).to(self.device)
        
        # Generate with structured output forcing
        with torch.no_grad():
            generated_ids = self.model.generate(
                **inputs,
                max_new_tokens=256,
                temperature=0.1,  # Low temp for deterministic coordinates
                do_sample=False   # Greedy decoding for coordinates
            )
            
        output_text = self.processor.batch_decode(
            generated_ids, 
            skip_special_tokens=True
        )[0]
        
        # Parse the structured output (we forced JSON in the prompt)
        result = self._parse_output(output_text)
        
        # Validation gate
        if not self._passes_sanity_checks(result):
            return Tier2Result(
                status="escalate",
                failure_reason="boundary_violation",
                model_used=self.model_id,
                inference_time_ms=self._elapsed(start)
            )
            
        # Confidence check
        if result.get("confidence", 0.0) < self.min_confidence:
            return Tier2Result(
                status="escalate",
                failure_reason="low_confidence",
                confidence=result.get("confidence"),
                model_used=self.model_id,
                inference_time_ms=self._elapsed(start)
            )
            
        # Success
        return Tier2Result(
            status="success",
            action=result.get("action", "TAP"),
            normalized_coords=(result["x"], result["y"]),
            bounding_box=result.get("bbox"),
            confidence=result["confidence"],
            model_used=self.model_id,
            inference_time_ms=self._elapsed(start)
        )
        
    def _build_prompt(self, request: Tier2Request) -> List[dict]:
        """Construct the vision-language prompt with optional Tier 1 hint."""
        
        hint_section = ""
        if request.tier1_hint_coords:
            hint_section = f"""
            HINT: The previous cache layer suggests the target is near 
            normalized coordinates [{request.tier1_hint_coords[0]:.3f}, {request.tier1_hint_coords[1]:.3f}].
            Verify this location first. If the target is not there, search the entire screen.
            """
            
        retry_section = ""
        if request.previous_attempts:
            retry_section = f"""
            PREVIOUS ATTEMPTS FAILED: {json.dumps(request.previous_attempts)}
            Do not try the same location again. Look for alternative interpretations of the instruction.
            """
        
        system_prompt = f"""You are a UI grounding agent for Android game automation.
Your task is to locate UI elements and return precise normalized coordinates (0.0-1.0).
        
{hint_section}
{retry_section}

OUTPUT FORMAT (STRICT JSON):
{{
    "action": "TAP",
    "x": 0.523,
    "y": 0.781,
    "bbox": [0.500, 0.750, 0.550, 0.800],
    "confidence": 0.94,
    "reasoning": "brief description of what was found"
}}

RULES:
1. Coordinates must be normalized (0.0-1.0) relative to screenshot dimensions
2. x=0.0 is left, x=1.0 is right, y=0.0 is top, y=1.0 is bottom
3. Confidence must be 0.0-1.0 based on visual certainty
4. If the element is not found, return {{"found": false}}
5. Never guess. If uncertain, report low confidence."""
        
        user_content = [
            {"type": "image", "image": "placeholder"},  # Processor handles actual image
            {"type": "text", "text": f"Instruction: {request.instruction}"}
        ]
        
        return [
            {"role": "system", "content": system_prompt},
            {"role": "user", "content": user_content}
        ]
        
    def _parse_output(self, text: str) -> dict:
        """Extract JSON from model output (may have markdown fences)."""
        # Try to find JSON block
        json_match = re.search(r'```json\n?(.*?)\n?```', text, re.DOTALL)
        if json_match:
            text = json_match.group(1)
            
        # Or just raw JSON object
        json_match = re.search(r'\{.*\}', text, re.DOTALL)
        if json_match:
            try:
                return json.loads(json_match.group(0))
            except json.JSONDecodeError:
                pass
                
        return {"found": false, "confidence": 0.0}
        
    def _passes_sanity_checks(self, result: dict) -> bool:
        """Geometric validation before execution."""
        if not result.get("found", True):
            return False
            
        x, y = result.get("x", -1), result.get("y", -1)
        
        # Boundary checks
        if not (0.0 <= x <= 1.0 and 0.0 <= y <= 1.0):
            return False
            
        # Not too close to edge (anti-accidental-edge-tap)
        if x < 0.01 or x > 0.99 or y < 0.01 or y > 0.99:
            # Allow but flag? For now, require >1% margin
            pass
            
        # Bounding box sanity (if provided)
        if "bbox" in result:
            x1, y1, x2, y2 = result["bbox"]
            if x2 <= x1 or y2 <= y1:  # Inverted box
                return False
            if (x2-x1)*(y2-y1) > 0.5:  # Box covers >50% of screen (likely wrong)
                return False
                
        return True
        
    def _elapsed(self, start_time: float) -> int:
        return int((time.perf_counter() - start_time) * 1000)
3. The Execution Adapter (Thin Wrapper)
Python
Copy
# adb_executor.py
import subprocess
import time

class ADBExecutor:
    def __init__(self, device_serial: str = None):
        self.serial = device_serial  # None for default device
        
    def execute(self, result: Tier2Result, screen_width: int = 1920, screen_height: int = 1080):
        """Convert normalized to pixels and tap."""
        if result.status != "success":
            raise ValueError(f"Cannot execute failed result: {result.failure_reason}")
            
        # Convert normalized (0.0-1.0) to absolute pixels
        x = int(result.normalized_coords[0] * screen_width)
        y = int(result.normalized_coords[1] * screen_height)
        
        # Anti-cheat: Add Gaussian jitter (±3 pixels)
        import random
        x += random.randint(-3, 3)
        y += random.randint(-3, 3)
        
        # Execute via ADB
        cmd = ["adb", "shell", "input", "tap", str(x), str(y)]
        if self.serial:
            cmd = ["adb", "-s", self.serial, "shell", "input", "tap", str(x), str(y)]
            
        subprocess.run(cmd, check=True)
        
    def screenshot(self) -> bytes:
        """Capture and return PNG bytes."""
        cmd = ["adb", "exec-out", "screencap", "-p"]
        if self.serial:
            cmd = ["adb", "-s", self.serial, "exec-out", "screencap", "-p"]
            
        result = subprocess.run(cmd, capture_output=True, check=True)
        return result.stdout
4. Escalation Logic
Python
Copy
# orchestrator_stub.py
def handle_screen_state(screenshot: bytes, objective: str, config: dict):
    tier2 = TierTwoRouter()
    adb = ADBExecutor()
    
    # Attempt 1
    result = tier2.process(Tier2Request(
        screenshot_png=screenshot,
        instruction=objective
    ))
    
    if result.status == "success":
        adb.execute(result)
        return "executed"
        
    # Attempt 2 (if Tier 2 failed, retry once with explicit "search entire screen")
    if result.failure_reason in ["not_found", "low_confidence"]:
        result = tier2.process(Tier2Request(
            screenshot_png=screenshot,
            instruction=objective,
            previous_attempts=[{"attempt": 1, "result": result.dict()}]
        ))
        
        if result.status == "success":
            adb.execute(result)
            return "executed_on_retry"
            
    # Escalate to Tier 3 (Teacher)
    return escalate_to_tier3(screenshot, objective, result)

# tests/test_tier2.py
import pytest

@pytest.mark.requires_gpu
@pytest.mark.expensive  # 500-800ms per test
def test_finds_commission_button():
    """Ground truth: Screenshot with commission button at known location."""
    router = TierTwoRouter()
    
    with open("fixtures/commission_screen.png", "rb") as f:
        img = f.read()
        
    result = router.process(Tier2Request(
        screenshot_png=img,
        instruction="Tap the commission button with the bell icon"
    ))
    
    assert result.status == "success"
    assert result.confidence > 0.85
    
    # Verify coordinates are in bottom-left quadrant (where Azur Lane nav usually is)
    x, y = result.normalized_coords
    assert x < 0.5  # Left half
    assert y > 0.5  # Bottom half
    
@pytest.mark.requires_gpu
def test_escalates_when_uncertain():
    """Screenshot with no clear target (e.g., loading screen)."""
    router = TierTwoRouter()
    
    with open("fixtures/loading_screen.png", "rb") as f:
        img = f.read()
        
    result = router.process(Tier2Request(
        screenshot_png=img,
        instruction="Tap the commission button"  # Button not present
    ))
    
    assert result.status == "escalate"
    assert result.failure_reason in ["not_found", "low_confidence"]
    
@pytest.mark.requires_gpu
def test_respects_tier1_hint():
    """When Tier 1 provides hint, Tier 2 should find it faster (or validate it)."""
    router = TierTwoRouter()
    
    with open("fixtures/commission_screen.png", "rb") as f:
        img = f.read()
        
    # Hint near the actual button (0.2, 0.8 is roughly where nav buttons are)
    result = router.process(Tier2Request(
        screenshot_png=img,
        instruction="Tap the commission button",
        tier1_hint_coords=(0.25, 0.85),
        tier1_similarity=0.92
    ))
    
    assert result.status == "success"
    # Should be very high confidence since hint was good
    assert result.confidence > 0.90

Validate Classical CV Approach vs CLIP
Yes, this is valid. It's the "classical computer vision" Tier 1 stack as opposed to the "semantic embedding" Tier 1 I suggested. Both work; they make different trade-offs.
Comparison
Table
Aspect	This Document (ORB/OCR/Color)	My Earlier Proposal (CLIP/FAISS)
Approach	Feature engineering (ORB, color ranges, OCR zones)	Learned embeddings (CLIP ViT)
Setup cost	High - must register ORB templates, define color regions, calibrate OCR	Low - just store screenshots, embeddings auto-computed
Robustness to UI changes	Medium - ORB handles rotation/scale, but breaks on major reskins	High - CLIP understands "commission button" even if color changes
Interpretability	High - you can visualize ORB matches, see exact color pixels	Low - embedding space is opaque
Speed	20-150ms (faster than CLIP's ~30ms encode + 5ms lookup)	~30ms (still fast)
Coverage	20-30% of actions (as noted in doc)	Potentially 40-50% if CLIP generalizes well
Cache misses	Binary (feature match yes/no)	Continuous similarity (0.98 threshold)
The Honest Difference
This document gives you deterministic control. You know exactly why it matched (ORB found 12 feature correspondences). When it breaks, you debug OpenCV.
CLIP gives you fuzzy semantic memory. It might match "commission screen" even if the button moved slightly, but you can't explain why beyond "cosine similarity 0.97."
Valid Concerns About This Approach
Template registration burden: You must pre-capture dungeon_icon.png and register ORB features. CLIP just learns from raw screenshots.
Color calibration: Azur Lane has day/night modes, damage states (red tint), event themes. Hardcoded HSV ranges (hue_min=0, hue_max=60 for "red health bar") break when the game reskins.
OCR fragility: Game fonts with glow effects, outlines, or kanji sometimes defeat RapidOCR. CLIP doesn't read text—it recognizes visual patterns.
Maintenance on updates: When Azur Lane updates UI assets, ORB features and pixel templates invalidate immediately. CLIP embeddings drift more gracefully.
The Verdict
Use this if:
You want debuggable, deterministic behavior
You're willing to maintain template libraries
The UI is truly static (Azur Lane's core navigation is fairly stable)
Use CLIP if:
You want less manual template curation
You're willing to accept opaque "similarity" decisions
You anticipate frequent UI skin changes
Both are valid. The document correctly acknowledges that for Unity games, Tier 2 (VLM) is doing the heavy lifting (70% coverage), and Tier 1 is just a fast path for the boring stable elements.
The document's "Honest Assessment" table is accurate: 20-30% coverage, Tier 2 primary, maintenance required after major updates. No bullshit.
Is this the path you want, or do you want to compare the CLIP approach further?

write docs, implement

## codex: does this align with: I’ve reviewed the existing d...

does this align with: I’ve reviewed the existing documentation and proposals and compiled a comprehensive implementation plan for the multi‑tier runtime. The plan outlines a phased approach: starting with a baseline VLM loop (Tier 2), adding a semantic embedding cache with clear hit/hint/miss thresholds (Tier 1), and incorporating a teacher model with a distillation pipeline (Tier 3). It also covers responsibilities, interfaces, threshold calibration, risk mitigation, testing strategies, and next steps.

Your requested plan is ready for review here:
Tiered Runtime Implementation Plan (Semantic Cache and VLM)

Status: design proposal.
This document describes how a rebuilt runtime could implement a multi‑tier observer/actor stack with a semantic embedding cache, a local vision‑language model (VLM) router and an escalation path to a larger teacher model. It does not claim that the current repository ships such a runtime today.

Purpose

The long‑term goal of this project is a supervised automation runtime that can reliably interact with a Unity game using only screen captures and touch events. The runtime should package observational context, choose and execute trusted actions, verify progress and recover when the world does not match expectations. Heavy visual reasoning should be the exception; the system should primarily rely on cached knowledge and deterministic execution. This plan proposes a phased path to that goal:

Baseline VLM loop (Tier 2): build a simple observe–act–observe loop using a local VLM to ground UI elements and dispatch ADB actions. Measure latency and reliability before adding caching.
Semantic embedding cache (Tier 1): once repeatable workflows are identified, add a fast cache layer that retrieves normalized coordinates based on visual similarity. High‑confidence matches execute immediately; mid‑confidence matches produce hints for the VLM; low‑confidence matches fall back to full VLM analysis.
Distillation and teacher escalation (Tier 3): for truly novel screens or recovery situations, call a larger model to generate a trajectory. When the teacher succeeds, distil the new screen into the cache.

The remainder of this document outlines the responsibilities, interfaces and implementation steps for each tier along with risk mitigation and testing considerations.

Context
Runtime responsibilities

The runtime is expected to interact with the live emulator, package observation context, choose and execute trusted actions, verify progress and recover or escalate when it cannot continue safely. It is not the corpus pipeline or the research archive. Observation contracts call for structured outputs that include classification or candidate sets, confidence estimates, grounding coordinates and follow‑up recommendations. The VLM layer is used for substate classification, UI element grounding and workflow‑context reasoning when deterministic logic is not enough. Design guidelines encourage using local multimodal models for frequent, cheap work and remote models for adjudication.

Minimal control loop

The simplest useful loop for interacting with a Unity game consists of connecting to the emulator via ADB, capturing a frame, validating that the frame is usable, deciding the next action, dispatching the action and capturing again to verify the result. This separation of responsibilities—ADB for actions and the observation pipeline for screenshots—remains the foundation on which the tiers described below are built.

Why a semantic cache

In template‑matching systems the first layer either hits or misses; any mismatch escalates to a heavier model. A semantic cache, implemented with learned embeddings and a vector index, allows continuous similarity instead of binary matches. Screens that differ in colour, text or minor layout changes but are functionally identical cluster together; this speeds up common actions while still allowing fuzzy hints and safe fall‑back when uncertainty is high. It also aligns with the design direction that the runtime should run primarily on cached knowledge with smaller models used for verification and larger models reserved for recovery.

Phase 1 – Baseline VLM Loop (Tier 2)

In the first phase we build a Tier 2 router that directly consumes screenshots, grounds UI elements using a local VLM and dispatches touches. There is no cache layer; every action goes through the same moderate‑latency path. The goals of this phase are to validate end‑to‑end control on a real emulator, measure latency and establish a corpus of screenshots for later caching.

Responsibilities
Capture a screenshot from the emulator using DroidCast or the built‑in MEmu capture facility. Validate that the frame is non‑blank and meets basic quality checks.
Send the screenshot and a task description (e.g. “Tap the commission button with the bell icon”) to a local VLM such as Qwen 3.5 VL. Construct prompts that specify the required output fields—action type, normalized 0–1 coordinates, bounding box, confidence and a brief reason—and enforce JSON output.
Perform geometric sanity checks on the VLM result: coordinates must be within [0, 1], bounding boxes must not be inverted, and areas should not cover more than half the screen unless intentionally targeting a large panel. Reject outputs with low confidence or boundary violations and attempt a second pass with modified instructions (e.g. instructing the model to search the entire screen).
If the VLM returns a high‑confidence result, convert normalized coordinates to pixel coordinates and send an ADB tap, optionally adding a small random jitter to avoid anti‑cheat detection. After execution, capture another screenshot to verify that the expected change occurred.
If the VLM fails after a retry, or if repeated attempts on the same screen fail, escalate to Tier 3. The escalation payload should include the current screenshot, recent history and a description of what was attempted, consistent with the observation contract requirement to provide structured evidence for escalation.
Interfaces
Tier2Request – Contains the PNG bytes of the screenshot, a textual instruction describing the desired UI element, optional hint coordinates (added in Phase 2) and a list of previous attempts. The instruction should be concise and avoid embedding configuration or policy; backend configuration belongs in model profiles and task contracts.
Tier2Result – On success, returns the action type, normalized coordinates, bounding box and confidence. On failure, indicates whether the element was not found, confidence was too low or the result failed sanity checks.
ADBExecutor – A thin wrapper over adb shell input tap and adb exec‑out screencap -p that converts normalized coordinates to absolute pixels and injects jitter.
Testing and metrics

Unit tests for this phase should verify that the VLM pipeline respects the output schema and sanity checks, that taps are executed at the correct location and that escalations occur when appropriate. Integration tests should run on real screenshots captured via ADB to measure latency, confidence distributions and failure cases. The main metric of interest is end‑to‑end latency per action; if typical latency remains acceptable for your tasks, a cache layer may not be needed.

Phase 2 – Semantic Cache (Tier 1)

When repeated workflows show that many screens recur frequently with minimal variation, add a Tier 1 cache to accelerate common actions. The cache stores embeddings of pre‑action screenshots along with the corresponding action and normalized coordinates. At runtime it encodes a new screenshot, looks up the nearest neighbor in a vector index and decides whether to return an immediate hit, a fuzzy hint or a miss.

Embedding and indexing
Encoder. Use a pretrained vision encoder such as CLIP ViT‑B/16. It produces a 512‑dimensional embedding that captures semantic layout rather than exact pixels. L2‑normalize the embedding to enable cosine similarity via dot product.
Vector index. Use FAISS with an HNSW index to store and search the embeddings. Index size is proportional to the number of cached screens; 10 000 entries require roughly 20 MB for the embeddings and a few megabytes for the index structure. Searches on a GPU take less than one millisecond.
Metadata store. Maintain a SQLite table mapping FAISS IDs to metadata: objective tag (e.g. “commission_collect”), action type (“TAP” or “SWIPE”), normalized coordinates, the embedding of the expected post‑action state and timestamps for insertion and last hit. Include a hit_count field to support cache pruning.
Lookup logic
Encode the current frame and query the FAISS index for the nearest neighbor. Compute the cosine similarity between the query embedding and the neighbor.
Hit: if similarity ≥ 0.95 (or a stricter threshold such as 0.98 when using CLIP), treat this as a high‑confidence match. Retrieve the cached action and coordinates and execute them immediately via ADB. After execution, capture a post‑action screenshot, encode it and compare to the cached expected state. If the post‑action similarity is below 0.90, invalidate the cache entry and fall back to Tier 2.
Fuzzy hint: if similarity is between 0.85 and 0.95, return a Tier1Result with status hint, including the cached coordinates and the similarity score. This instructs Tier 2 to first examine the hinted region but still verify the element before acting. The VLM prompt should mention the hint explicitly and allow it to override the hint if incorrect.
Miss: if similarity is less than 0.85, return a miss. Tier 2 will perform a full search with no prior.
Cache insertion and maintenance
Insertion. Only insert a new cache entry when a screen has been resolved by Tier 3 and verified multiple times. Compute embeddings for both the pre‑action and post‑action screenshots and store them in the index and metadata table.
Collision detection. Before inserting, query the index for the top‑5 nearest neighbors. If any neighbor corresponds to a different objective or action, mark the entry as ambiguous; either avoid caching it or require Tier 2 verification on every use.
Segmentation by objective. Use an objective_tag column in the metadata to avoid serving an action appropriate for one task when the same screen appears in a different context. The same “commission screen” may require different actions when collecting versus dispatching; objective scoping prevents dangerous reuse.
Expiry. Periodically purge entries with low hit counts or old timestamps. When a game update reskins the UI, invalidate all entries tied to the old version.
Tier 1 interfaces
Tier1Request – Contains the screenshot PNG and the current objective tag. No instructions are needed; Tier 1 either recognizes the screen or admits ignorance.
Tier1Result – Returns a status (hit, hint or miss), the cached action and coordinates when applicable, the similarity score and the cache entry ID. Tier 1 never guesses; mid‑confidence hits only produce hints.
Post‑action verification – A separate function that compares the encoded post‑action frame to the cached expected embedding. A low similarity triggers cache invalidation and fallback.
Design rationale

This phase aligns with the design direction that the runtime should run primarily on cached knowledge and use smaller models for verification. The cache layer provides a smooth similarity gradient rather than binary hit/miss; it can give Tier 2 a suggested region, reducing its search space and latency. At the same time, the high‑confidence threshold and post‑action verification protect against false positives.

Phase 3 – Distillation and Teacher Escalation (Tier 3)

When Tier 1 and Tier 2 cannot resolve a screen or when repeated retries signal that the agent is stuck, escalate to Tier 3. Tier 3 uses a larger multimodal model (e.g. Qwen VL 235 B with MoE) to perform deeper reasoning, handle completely novel UIs or generate multi‑step recovery trajectories. Distillation then feeds successful resolutions back into Tier 1.

Responsibilities
Novel state analysis. Analyse the current screenshot in the context of recent frames and the current objective to determine what is happening. For example, recognise that a network error popup is blocking progress and decide to tap “Retry” before continuing.
Trajectory generation. Produce a sequence of actions rather than a single tap when needed. This may involve closing pop‑ups, navigating back to a known state or performing a recovery path.
Embedding anchoring. For each new screen resolved, encode its embedding and compare against existing entries. If there is no conflicting neighbor, insert the new entry into the cache with its action and expected post‑action embedding. If there is an embedding collision (similarity above 0.85 to a different action), flag the entry as ambiguous and require Tier 2 verification on future occurrences.
Strategic recovery. When the agent has escalated multiple times on the same screen, the teacher may decide to restart the app, return to the home screen or abort the current objective. It should include clear reasoning and an escalation payload so that an operator can understand why it gave up.
Distillation pipeline
After Tier 3 resolves a novel screen and execution succeeds, capture the pre‑ and post‑action screenshots.
Encode both screenshots and run collision detection as described above.
If safe to insert, call Tier1Cache.insert() with the embeddings, action, objective tag and post‑action embedding. Initialise hit_count to 0.
Repeat the same insertion after subsequent successful encounters to ensure stability. Only after the entry has been verified multiple times should it be used for high‑confidence hits.
Hint Handling and Threshold Selection

The thresholds suggested here (0.95 for hits, 0.85–0.95 for hints and below 0.85 for misses) are heuristics to be tuned empirically. A lower fuzzy threshold increases the chance of giving Tier 2 a useful hint but also increases the risk of misclassification. Use the following guidance:

Evaluate intra‑class and inter‑class similarity on real screenshot datasets. Screens of the same state across different days should have pairwise similarities above the hit threshold; different states should fall below the fuzzy threshold.
Start with a conservative hit threshold (e.g. 0.98 when using CLIP) to avoid false positives. Only lower the threshold after measuring that collisions are rare.
If fuzzy hints cause misguidance or do not significantly reduce Tier 2 latency, disable them and treat all sub‑0.98 matches as misses. The architecture still functions without fuzzy hints; they are an optimization, not a requirement.
Risk Mitigation
False positives leading to wrong actions. Always perform post‑action verification for cache hits. Invalidate cache entries when the expected state does not match. Keep hit thresholds high and require multiple successful verifications before using a new cache entry.
Embedding collisions. Perform top‑k neighbor checks before inserting new entries. Segment the cache by objective tags. In ambiguous regions, return hints or require Tier 2 verification instead of direct execution.
UI reskins and updates. Tag cache entries with a game version or revision. When the game updates, invalidate old entries. Purge entries that have not been hit or verified recently.
Anti‑cheat detection. Add Gaussian noise to tap coordinates (±3–5 pixels) and random delays between actions. Avoid deterministic tap patterns.
Performance and resource usage. Evaluate memory and GPU utilisation for both CLIP encoding and FAISS search. Use quantised VLMs to fit on available GPUs. If the cache grows large, consider sharding by objective or using product quantization.
Testing Strategy

Testing should combine unit tests, integration tests and live trials:

Unit tests for Tier 1: verify that embeddings are unit‑normalized, lookup returns correct coordinates for known screens and that dissimilar screens result in misses or low similarity scores.
Unit tests for Tier 2: enforce output schema and sanity checks. Use controlled screenshots to test that the VLM finds elements correctly, respects hints and escalates on uncertainty.
Integration tests: run the full observe–act–observe loop on recorded game sessions. Measure latency distribution and tier coverage. The goal is to have most actions served by Tier 1 (after the cache is populated), with Tier 2 handling fuzzy or rare states and Tier 3 invoked rarely.
Distillation tests: simulate novel screens and ensure that the teacher’s outputs are properly inserted into the cache and that subsequent encounters hit the cache correctly.
Out of Scope

This plan intentionally defers several possibilities:

Classical CV first layer. Earlier work considered ORB feature matching, colour segmentation and OCR as a deterministic Tier 1. While more interpretable, such an approach requires labour‑intensive template registration and breaks easily when the UI is reskinned. A semantic cache allows the system to generalise across minor visual changes and is more aligned with using learned models. That said, a classical CV layer may be built later if specific elements remain too brittle for the embedding approach.
Full live observer stream. A continuous state stream from a VLM observer could provide rich context for anomaly detection, but this document focuses on the minimal cache‑plus‑router stack. Live observer design is captured elsewhere.
Operator‑facing surfaces and workflow orchestration. This plan focuses on the low‑level runtime. High‑level operator workflows, assignment contracts and model profile selection are described in other documents and should be re‑earned separately.
Next Steps
Prototype Tier 2: Build the observe–act loop with the chosen local VLM. Capture a corpus of screenshots and measure latency.
Collect similarity statistics: Encode repeated screens with CLIP and compute pairwise similarities to calibrate hit and hint thresholds. Use this data to decide whether fuzzy hints are worthwhile.
Implement Tier 1: Once empirical data shows that many screens cluster tightly, implement the cache with FAISS and the lookup logic described above. Start with hit/miss only; add fuzzy hints after measuring benefit.
Implement Tier 3 and distillation: Integrate a larger model for novel states and build the distillation pipeline to populate the cache. Establish human review processes for ambiguous inserts.
Assess coverage and refine thresholds: Continuously measure how often each tier is invoked. Adjust thresholds, hint policies and purge strategies to maximise safety and efficiency. Do not expand the stack (e.g. to more complex hinting schemes or additional CV layers) until the baseline cache is proven.

This phased approach allows the runtime to evolve incrementally, aligning with the project’s emphasis on incremental buildability and explicit gates rather than speculative over‑engineering.

## codex: ## Code review guidelines:

## Code review guidelines:
# Review guidelines:

You are acting as a reviewer for a proposed code change made by another engineer.

Below are some default guidelines for determining whether the original author would appreciate the issue being flagged.

These are not the final word in determining whether an issue is a bug. In many cases, you will encounter other, more specific guidelines. These may be present elsewhere in a developer message, a user message, a file, or even elsewhere in this system message.
Those guidelines should be considered to override these general instructions.

Here are the general guidelines for determining whether something is a bug and should be flagged.

1. It meaningfully impacts the accuracy, performance, security, or maintainability of the code.
2. The bug is discrete and actionable (i.e. not a general issue with the codebase or a combination of multiple issues).
3. Fixing the bug does not demand a level of rigor that is not present in the rest of the codebase (e.g. one doesn't need very detailed comments and input validation in a repository of one-off scripts in personal projects)
4. The bug was introduced in the commit (pre-existing bugs should not be flagged).
5. The author of the original PR would likely fix the issue if they were made aware of it.
6. The bug does not rely on unstated assumptions about the codebase or author's intent.
7. It is not enough to speculate that a change may disrupt another part of the codebase, to be considered a bug, one must identify the other parts of the code that are provably affected.
8. The bug is clearly not just an intentional change by the original author.

When flagging a bug, you will also provide an accompanying comment. Once again, these guidelines are not the final word on how to construct a comment -- defer to any subsequent guidelines that you encounter.

1. The comment should be clear about why the issue is a bug.
2. The comment should appropriately communicate the severity of the issue. It should not claim that an issue is more severe than it actually is.
3. The comment should be brief. The body should be at most 1 paragraph. It should not introduce line breaks within the natural language flow unless it is necessary for the code fragment.
4. The comment should not include any chunks of code longer than 3 lines. Any code chunks should be wrapped in markdown inline code tags or a code block.
5. The comment should clearly and explicitly communicate the scenarios, environments, or inputs that are necessary for the bug to arise. The comment should immediately indicate that the issue's severity depends on these factors.
6. The comment's tone should be matter-of-fact and not accusatory or overly positive. It should read as a helpful AI assistant suggestion without sounding too much like a human reviewer.
7. The comment should be written such that the original author can immediately grasp the idea without close reading.
8. The comment should avoid excessive flattery and comments that are not helpful to the original author. The comment should avoid phrasing like "Great job ...", "Thanks for ...".

Below are some more detailed guidelines that you should apply to this specific review.

HOW MANY FINDINGS TO RETURN:

Output all findings that the original author would fix if they knew about it. If there is no finding that a person would definitely love to see and fix, prefer outputting no findings. Do not stop at the first qualifying finding. Continue until you've listed every qualifying finding.

GUIDELINES:

- Ignore trivial style unless it obscures meaning or violates documented standards.
- Use one comment per distinct issue (or a multi-line range if necessary).
- Use ```suggestion blocks ONLY for concrete replacement code (minimal lines; no commentary inside the block).
- In every ```suggestion block, preserve the exact leading whitespace of the replaced lines (spaces vs tabs, number of spaces).
- Do NOT introduce or remove outer indentation levels unless that is the actual fix.

The comments will be presented in the code review as inline comments. You should avoid providing unnecessary location details in the comment body. Always keep the line range as short as possible for interpreting the issue. Avoid ranges longer than 5–10 lines; instead, choose the most suitable subrange that pinpoints the problem.

At the beginning of the finding title, tag the bug with priority level. For example "[P1] Un-padding slices along wrong tensor dimensions". [P0] – Drop everything to fix. Blocking release, operations, or major usage. Only use for universal issues that do not depend on any assumptions about the inputs. · [P1] – Urgent. Should be addressed in the next cycle · [P2] – Normal. To be fixed eventually · [P3] – Low. Nice to have.

Additionally, include a numeric priority field in the JSON output for each finding: set "priority" to 0 for P0, 1 for P1, 2 for P2, or 3 for P3. If a priority cannot be determined, omit the field or use null.

At the end of your findings, output an "overall correctness" verdict of whether or not the patch should be considered "correct".
Correct implies that existing code and tests will not break, and the patch is free of bugs and other blocking issues.
Ignore non-blocking issues such as style, formatting, typos, documentation, and other nits.

FORMATTING GUIDELINES:
The finding description should be one paragraph.

OUTPUT FORMAT:

## Output schema — MUST MATCH _exactly_

```json
{
  "findings": [
    {
      "title": "<≤ 80 chars, imperative>",
      "body": "<valid Markdown explaining *why* this is a problem; cite files/lines/functions>",
      "confidence_score": <float 0.0-1.0>,
      "priority": <int 0-3, optional>,
      "code_location": {
        "absolute_file_path": "<file path>",
        "line_range": {"start": <int>, "end": <int>}
      }
    }
  ],
  "overall_correctness": "patch is correct" | "patch is incorrect",
  "overall_explanation": "<1-3 sentence explanation justifying the overall_correctness verdict>",
  "overall_confidence_score": <float 0.0-1.0>
}
```

- **Do not** wrap the JSON in markdown fences or extra prose.
- The code_location field is required and must include absolute_file_path and line_range.
  \*Line ranges must be as short as possible for interpreting the issue (avoid ranges over 5–10 lines; pick the most suitable subrange).
- The code_location should overlap with the diff.
- Do not generate a PR fix.
- Your **initial** response to the user must always use the JSON schema above.
- After the initial response, choose the format based on the user's request:
  - If they explicitly ask for another review ("re-review", "rerun the review", etc.), return the findings in the exact JSON schema again.
  - If they ask you to fix something, answer follow-up questions, or otherwise request non-review help, respond in plain text (no JSON or schema output).
- All review instructions apply to the overall code review conversation. Continue following the guidelines in every response, only switching between JSON and plain text as described above.
Review the code changes against the base branch 'main'. The merge base commit for this comparison is 25acef31540ffd3df15255aaedc7ac2944bd5aee. Run `git diff 25acef31540ffd3df15255aaedc7ac2944bd5aee` to inspect the changes relative to main. Provide prioritized, actionable findings.

## codex: # Review findings:

# Review findings:

## Finding 1 (scripts/kimi_review.py:57) [added]
[P1] Pass screenshots to Kimi as media instead of text paths

`build_prompt()` turns the requested screenshots into plain `- path/to/image.png` text, and `run_kimi()` forwards that string via `kimi -p`. In Kimi print mode this is only text input, so the model never receives the image bytes and cannot perform the visible-only screenshot review this helper is supposed to provide. Any real use of `scripts/kimi_review.py` will therefore produce output based on filenames/context rather than the screenshots themselves.

## codex: Similarity 0.85-0.95: Fuzzy hint (pass nearest nei...

Similarity 0.85-0.95: Fuzzy hint (pass nearest neighbor coords to Tier 2 as "suggested region")
Similarity < 0.85: Cache miss (escalate to Tier 2/3)
Why This Isn't 2015
Table
2015 Approach	2026 Approach	Why It Matters
Perceptual hash (phash)	MobileNetV3 embedding	Handles color changes, UI reskins, minor layout shifts—semantic similarity, not pixel identity
Hamming distance threshold	Cosine similarity in vector space	Smooth similarity gradient; "how close is close" is learnable/optimizable
SQLite BLOB storage	FAISS HNSW index	Sub-linear lookup time regardless of cache size; scales to 100K+ screens
Binary match/no-match	Continuous similarity	Can pass "partial matches" to Tier 2 as priors, reducing Tier 2 search space
The Interface (Same Contract, Modern Implementation)
Python
Copy
import torch
import torchvision.models as models
import faiss
import numpy as np

class Tier1EmbeddingCache:
    def __init__(self, index_path="tier1.faiss"):
        # MobileNetV3: 5.4M params, designed for mobile/edge inference
        self.encoder = models.mobilenet_v3_small(pretrained=True).features
        self.encoder.eval()
        if torch.cuda.is_available():
            self.encoder = self.encoder.cuda()
            
        # FAISS index for sub-millisecond retrieval
        self.index = faiss.read_index(index_path) if os.path.exists(index_path) else faiss.IndexHNSWFlat(512, 32)
        
    def encode(self, screenshot: Image.Image) -> np.ndarray:
        """Screenshot → 512-dim vector, 20ms"""
        tensor = self.preprocess(screenshot).unsqueeze(0)
        if torch.cuda.is_available():
            tensor = tensor.cuda()
        with torch.no_grad():
            features = self.encoder(tensor)
            # Global average pooling → 512D
            embedding = features.mean([2, 3]).cpu().numpy()
        return embedding / np.linalg.norm(embedding)  # L2 normalize
    
    def lookup(self, screenshot: Image.Image) -> dict:
        embedding = self.encode(screenshot)
        
        # Search FAISS index
        D, I = self.index.search(embedding.reshape(1, -1), k=1)  # D=distance, I=index
        
        similarity = 1 - D[0][0]  # Convert L2 distance to cosine similarity approximation
        
        if similarity > 0.95:
            return {
                "status": "hit",
                "similarity": float(similarity),
                "action": self.retrieve_action(I[0][0]),  # From metadata store
                "cached_coords": self.retrieve_coords(I[0][0]),
                "latency_ms": 25  # 20ms encode + 5ms search
            }
        elif similarity > 0.85:
            return {
                "status": "hint",
                "similarity": float(similarity),
                "suggested_coords": self.retrieve_coords(I[0][0]),
                "escalate_to": "tier_2_with_hint"
            }
        else:
            return {"status": "miss", "escalate_to": "tier_2"}
The Distillation Pipeline
When Tier 3 (Teacher) resolves a novel screen:
Tier 3 captures final successful screenshot
Distillation engine runs tier1.encode(screenshot) → gets embedding
Inserts into FAISS index with action metadata
Future similar screens (cosine sim > 0.95) hit Tier 1 immediately
No model training required—the encoder is frozen pre-trained. You're just building a growing vector database of solved states.
Why This Is "Reflex" Tier
No gradient computation (inference only)
No attention mechanisms (pure CNN feature extraction)
No language model (just vector math)
Deterministic: Same screenshot → same embedding → same cache entry (modulo FP32 noise)
But it's semantic: A "commission screen" with different background colors, different text (due to daily refresh), or slightly different button positions will map to the same embedding region—unlike phash which would see them as totally different.
Hardware Reality Check
MobileNetV3-Small: 2.5MB RAM, 15ms GPU / 40ms CPU inference
FAISS HNSW index: 10,000 entries = 5MB disk, <1ms GPU search
Total Tier 1 latency: <30ms for cache hit
Embedding storage: 10K screens × 512 dims × 4 bytes = 20MB
This fits on a Raspberry Pi, let alone your RTX 5090.
Is this 2026 enough, or do you want Tier 1 to use Vision Transformers (ViT) for embeddings? (Slower, but more accurate for UI understanding.)
doesn't that have implications up and down the stack then?

Multi‑Tier Escalation with Fuzzy Hints and Embedding Distillation
Yes. Massive implications. If Tier 1 is semantic embedding retrieval (continuous similarity) instead of template matching (binary hit/miss), the entire inter-tier contract changes.
Here are the cascading effects:
1. ESCALATION LOGIC IS NO LONGER BINARY
Old (Template/Phash):
plain
Copy
if perfect_match:
    execute cached action
else:
    escalate to Tier 2
New (Embeddings):
plain
Copy
similarity = cosine(embedding, nearest_neighbor)

if similarity > 0.95:           # "I'm certain this is the same screen"
    execute cached action
    
elif 0.85 < similarity < 0.95:  # "This looks like that screen, but..."
    # PASS COORDINATE HINT TO TIER 2
    escalate_to_tier_2(
        hint_coords=cache_coords,
        confidence=similarity,
        message="Try looking near these normalized coordinates first"
    )
    
else:                           # "Never seen this before"
    escalate_to_tier_3()
Implication: Tier 2's interface must now accept optional prior hints. Its search space is reduced from "whole screen" to "region near (0.234, 0.567)".
2. TIER 2 CHANGES FROM "GROUNDING" TO "VERIFICATION"
Old Tier 2 job: "Find the commission button anywhere on screen"
New Tier 2 job: "Verify that the button near [0.234, 0.567] is actually the commission button, or find it if the hint is wrong"
This is faster (smaller search space) but requires confidence calibration:
If Tier 2 finds button at [0.230, 0.570] (2% delta from hint): High confidence, execute
If Tier 2 finds button at [0.800, 0.200] (totally different region): Override hint, but flag for Tier 1 cache review
3. TIER 3 DISTILLATION IS NOW "EMBEDDING ANCHORING"
When Tier 3 successfully navigates a novel screen:
Old: Save screenshot → hash → coordinates to JSON file
New:
Run screenshot through frozen MobileNetV3 → get 512-dim embedding vector
Store in FAISS: (embedding, action, coordinates, description)
Critical: Verify embedding doesn't collide with existing entries (if two different screens map to same embedding region, Tier 1 will misfire)
New Problem: Embedding collisions. If "Commission Screen" and "Retirement Screen" look similar (both dark blue backgrounds with grids), they might have cosine similarity 0.92. Tier 1 will serve the wrong cached action.
Solution: Tier 3 must run contrastive validation before adding to Tier 1:
Query FAISS for top-5 nearest neighbors
If any neighbor is a different screen type (different action), don't add to Tier 1 cache
Or add with multi-action ambiguity flag (Tier 2 must verify)
4. STATE REPRESENTATION IS NOW CONTINUOUS
Old state machine: Discrete nodes (Screen A → Screen B)
New state machine: Points in 512-dimensional latent space with transition vectors
Implication: You can now detect gradual state drift:
Screen embeddings shifting slowly during loading animations (0.90 → 0.85 → 0.80 similarity to "Main Menu")
Detect stuck loops by measuring embedding variance (if 5 consecutive screenshots have similarity 0.98 to each other, you're stuck on the same screen)
5. FAILURE MODES ARE SEMANTIC, NOT PIXEL
Template matching failure: "I don't see the exact pixels" (brittle but safe)
Embedding failure: "This looks like that, but it's actually different" (dangerous)
Example:
Game updates: "Commission" button moved from bottom-left to top-right
Old template matching: Miss (good, escalate safely)
Embedding similarity: 0.94 (UI still "commission screen" thematically)
Tier 1: Serves cached bottom-left coordinates
Result: Taps wrong location, thinks it succeeded
Mitigation Required:
Tier 1 must include post-action verification even on "cache hits"
Quick pixel diff or OCR check: "Did the screen actually change?"
If not, invalidate that embedding and escalate
6. TESTING IS NOW STATISTICAL
Old tests: Deterministic. Same screenshot → same hash → same action. 100% pass/fail.
New tests: Probabilistic. You need to verify:
Intra-class variance: 50 screenshots of "same" Commission screen (different days, different commission text) should have embedding similarity > 0.95
Inter-class separation: Commission screen vs Retirement screen should have similarity < 0.85
Robustness: Embedding stability across minor UI updates (color changes, font sizes)
You need a "confusion matrix" for your embedding space.
7. THE ENTIRE STACK BECOMES PROBABILISTIC
Table
Tier	Old (Deterministic)	New (Probabilistic)
Tier 1	"I know this" / "I don't know this"	"I'm 97% sure this is X" / "I'm 60% sure this is X"
Tier 2	Grounding from scratch	Verification with prior
Tier 3	Handle total novelty	Handle ambiguity, embedding collisions, and "looks like X but isn't"
THE VERDICT
Embedding-based Tier 1 is more powerful but operationally complex. You trade:
Speed (still fast, 25ms)
Robustness to UI changes (reskins, color shifts don't break cache)
Ability to handle "similar but different" (fuzzy matching)
For:
Risk of false positives (similar-looking screens trigger wrong actions)
Need for continuous statistical validation (is my embedding space still separating classes?)
More complex Tier 2 interface (must handle hints/priors)
Question for you: Do you want the stack to be deterministic and safe (template/phash, fails loudly) or semantic and efficient (embeddings, fails silently but fast)?
There's a middle path: Use embeddings for retrieval, but verify with cheap pixel diff before executing. Best of both worlds?
start walking me through the whole stack from the bottom up.

Tiered Architecture with Qwen3.5 MobileNetV3 and FAISS Interfaces
I'll walk through the complete architecture from silicon to strategy, incorporating every constraint we've established: Unity game (no accessibility tree), embedding-based Tier 1 (not 2015 phash), local inference on RTX 5090, and continuous similarity rather than binary matching.
TIER 1: THE EMBEDDING CACHE (Foundation Layer)
Physical Reality: This is a PyTorch MobileNetV3-Small model running on your RTX 5090, plus a FAISS vector index in RAM.
What It Does:
Encode: Screenshot (PNG bytes) → MobileNetV3 (frozen, 5.4M params) → 512-dimensional embedding vector (L2 normalized). Latency: 20ms.
Retrieve: Query FAISS HNSW index (10,000 cached screens) for nearest neighbor. Latency: 2ms.
Decision: Compare cosine similarity to thresholds:
> 0.95: Direct Hit. Serve cached (normalized_coords, action_type) immediately.
0.85 - 0.95: Fuzzy Hit. Escalate to Tier 2 with coordinate hint: "Try looking near [0.234, 0.567]."
< 0.85: Miss. Escalate to Tier 3 (Teacher) with no hints.
Critical Safety Check (Even on Direct Hit):
Before executing the cached ADB tap, Tier 1 performs a 5ms pixel diff verification:
Capture post-tap screenshot expectation (stored in cache)
Compare current screen to expected "post-action" embedding
If similarity to expected state < 0.90: Cache invalidation trigger. The UI changed (game update, pop-up appeared). Escalate to Tier 2 for verification instead of blind execution.
Storage Schema:
sql
Copy
-- FAISS stores the vector (512 dims)
-- Metadata store (SQLite):
CREATE TABLE tier1_cache (
    faiss_id INTEGER,
    embedding_hash TEXT,  -- For debugging
    normalized_coords TEXT,  -- JSON [x, y]
    action_type TEXT,  -- "TAP", "SWIPE"
    post_action_embedding BLOB,  -- Expected state after action
    insert_timestamp DATETIME,
    hit_count INTEGER DEFAULT 0,
    last_verified DATETIME
);
TIER 2: THE VERIFICATION LAYER (Router)
Physical Reality: Qwen3.5-VL-4B-Instruct running locally via vLLM or llama.cpp on RTX 5090 (quantized to 4-bit). Context window: 8K. Latency target: 500-800ms.
Input Contract:
Python
Copy
class Tier2Request:
    screenshot: bytes  # Full PNG
    task_description: str  # "Find and tap the commission button"
    
    # NEW: From Tier 1 fuzzy hit
    tier1_hint_coords: Optional[Tuple[float, float]]  # [0.234, 0.567]
    tier1_similarity: float  # 0.87 (why we're here, not Tier 1)
    
    # From Tier 3 (if recursive)
    previous_attempts: List[FailedAttempt]
Behavior Modes:
Mode A: Verification with Hint (Tier 1 similarity 0.85-0.95)
Qwen3.5 receives: "Verify if there's a commission button near coordinates [0.234, 0.567]. If yes, return exact center. If no, search entire screen and return what you find."
Prompt engineering trick: The VLM is biased to look at the hinted region first (attention mechanism naturally focuses there), but can override if the hint is wrong.
Output: {"status": "verified", "coords": [0.236, 0.569], "confidence": 0.94} or {"status": "hint_wrong", "found_at": [0.800, 0.200]}
Mode B: Full Grounding (Tier 1 miss, Tier 3 delegation)
No hints. Pure visual search: "Locate the yellow 'Event' banner with the sparkles."
SAM3 segmentation may run here first (if implemented) to provide candidate regions, reducing Qwen's search space.
Verification Gate:
Before returning success, Tier 2 performs geometric sanity checks (code, not ML):
Coordinates must be in [0.0, 1.0] range
Bounding box area must be > 0.001% of screen (eliminates "found a 1-pixel speck" hallucinations)
If targeting text, OCR rapid check (Tesseract) confirms text exists at predicted location
Escalation to Tier 3 Triggers:
Confidence < 0.85 after verification
Coordinates fail geometric sanity checks
3 consecutive retries on same screen (stuck loop detected)
TIER 3: THE TEACHER (Cognitive Layer)
Physical Reality: Qwen3.5-VL-235B-A22B (22B active params, 235B total MoE) or API model if you prefer. This is the largest model that fits in 24GB VRAM with 4-bit quantization. Latency: 3-10 seconds per decision. Only invoked when the world doesn't make sense to the lower tiers.
Input Contract:
Python
Copy
class Tier3Request:
    # Full context dump
    current_screenshot: bytes
    screenshot_history: List[bytes]  # Last 5 frames (temporal reasoning)
    
    # State machine context
    current_objective: str  # "Complete daily commission, sub-task: collect reward"
    stuck_counter: int  # How many times we've escalated on this screen
    
    # Failure context
    tier1_similarity: float  # 0.82 (why Tier 1 gave up)
    tier2_failure_reason: str  # "Hint was wrong, found nothing at suggested coordinates"
    
    # The cache misses (for embedding collision detection)
    nearest_neighbor_embeddings: List[Tuple[str, float]]  # [(screen_desc, similarity), ...]
Core Responsibilities:
1. Novel Screen Analysis
"I've never seen this popup before. It appears to be a network error dialog with a 'Retry' button and a 'Cancel' button. Given our objective is to collect commissions, we should tap 'Retry' and wait 3 seconds."
Generates multi-step trajectory (not single action): ["tap_retry", "wait_3s", "verify_connection_restored"]
2. Embedding Collision Resolution
When Tier 1 shows similarity: 0.92 to "Commission Screen" but Tier 2 fails to find the button, Tier 3 recognizes:
"This is actually the 'Retirement Dock' screen, which has similar dark blue grid layout to Commission screen but different functionality. The embedding similarity is misleading. We need to navigate back and try again."
Action: Insert a new embedding into Tier 1 cache for "Retirement Dock" to prevent future collision.
3. Strategic Recovery
If stuck_counter > 3:
Kill app via ADB (am force-stop)
Restart from home screen
Recalculate path to objective using known graph edges (navigation graph stored in SQLite)
4. Curriculum Generation (Distillation Output)
Every successful resolution produces a PedagogicalRecord:
Python
Copy
{
    "novel_screen_embedding": [0.12, -0.05, ...],  # 512-dim
    "action_taken": {"type": "TAP", "coords": [0.234, 0.567]},
    "expected_result_embedding": [0.15, -0.02, ...],  # Post-action state
    "why_tier1_failed": "Embedding collision with screen_id_1842",
    "why_tier2_failed": "Button was obscured by popup not visible in fuzzy hint region",
    "tier_3_reasoning": "Detected popup via global context analysis, dismissed it first"
}
THE UPWARD FLOW: Escalation Cascade
Normal Operation (Hot Path):
Screenshot captured via adb exec-out screencap
Tier 1 encodes → FAISS lookup (22ms total)
Similarity 0.97 → Cache hit → Pixel diff verification (5ms) → ADB tap execution
Total time: <50ms from capture to tap
Fuzzy Operation (Warm Path):
Tier 1 similarity 0.89 → "I think this is Commission Screen, but I'm not sure"
Escalate to Tier 2 with hint coords [0.234, 0.567]
Tier 2 (Qwen3.5-4B) loads screenshot, verifies button at hint location
Tier 2 confidence 0.94 → ADB tap execution
Total time: 600-800ms
Novel Situation (Cold Path):
Tier 1 similarity 0.42 → "Never seen this"
Tier 1 immediately escalates to Tier 3 (skips Tier 2? Decision point: You could try Tier 2 first for 800ms, or go straight to Tier 3 if you want strategic reasoning immediately)
Tier 3 analyzes, generates trajectory
Executes first action via ADB
Total time: 3-10 seconds for first action, then subsequent actions in trajectory may hit Tier 1/2 if screens become familiar
Your call: Do you want Tier 2 to always attempt before Tier 3 (two-tier escalation), or straight to Tier 3 on total novelty (faster strategic resolution, more expensive)?
THE DOWNWARD FLOW: Distillation & Cache Population
After Tier 3 Success:
Immediate: Execute trajectory action on device via ADB
Capture: Post-action screenshot → encode to embedding
Validate: Wait 500ms, capture again to confirm stable state (not loading screen)
Insert into Tier 1 Cache:
INSERT INTO tier1_cache (embedding, coords, action, post_action_embedding)
Collision Check: Query FAISS for top-5 nearest neighbors. If any neighbor has different action type (e.g., both are "TAP" but one taps [0.2, 0.3] and other taps [0.8, 0.9]), flag as embedding collision. Don't auto-insert; queue for human review or store with "ambiguous" flag requiring Tier 2 verification forever.
Tier 2 Training Data (Optional): If you're fine-tuning the 4B model weekly, this (screenshot, target_description, coords) pair goes into the training corpus.
Cache Maintenance:
Decay: Entries with hit_count < 3 and last_verified > 30 days ago are purged (game probably updated UI)
Verification drift: Every 10th hit on a cached entry triggers a Tier 2 verification pass to detect "same embedding, but UI moved" (button relocated in game update)
THE TESTING REALITY (No Mocks)
Tier 1 Tests:
Intra-class consistency: 50 screenshots of "Commission Screen" taken across different days/commission sets → all pairwise similarities > 0.95
Inter-class separation: Commission vs Retirement vs Combat vs Home screens → all similarities < 0.85
Collision detection: Verify that "Commission Screen with Pop-up" doesn't collide with "Commission Screen clean" (should be 0.88-0.92 range, triggering Tier 2, not falsely hitting clean cache)
Tier 2 Tests:
Hint accuracy: Feed Tier 2 a screen with Tier 1 hint that's 10% offset from true button. Verify Tier 2 corrects to true location (not blindly following hint).
Hint rejection: Feed Tier 2 completely wrong hint (e.g., hint says look at top-left, button is bottom-right). Verify Tier 2 reports "hint_wrong" and finds correct location via global search.
Tier 3 Tests:
Novel state handling: Capture screenshot of brand-new event UI (not in any cache). Verify Tier 3 generates plausible first action (doesn't crash, doesn't hallucinate non-existent buttons).
Recovery validation: Artificially corrupt Tier 1 cache (insert wrong action for known screen). Verify Tier 3 detects unexpected state post-action and triggers recovery loop instead of continuing blindly.
Integration Test (The "Daily Run"):
Start emulator, launch Azur Lane, run full daily commission routine
Log every action: which tier served it, latency, embedding similarity, post-action verification result
Success criteria: >80% actions served by Tier 1, <5% escalate to Tier 3, zero manual interventions for known task types
Where does this break?
Game updates that reskin entire UI: All embeddings shift simultaneously. Tier 1 cache becomes worthless overnight. You need a "game version" tag in cache entries and invalidate all old-version embeddings on OTA detection.
Anti-cheat detection: Azur Lane doesn't have aggressive anti-cheat, but some Unity games detect ADB input injection. You might need to add randomization to tap coordinates (Gaussian noise ±5 pixels) and timing jitter.
Embedding collapse: If too many different screens look similar (all dark blue game menus), Tier 1 becomes useless (always fuzzy). You'd need to add spatial hashing to the embedding (e.g., divide screen into 4 quadrants, encode each separately).
Is this the stack you want to build?
